Urban Beauty & Public Space

A City That Looks Like It Believes in Itself

Empty lots, blank walls, and bare sidewalks tell every visitor and every potential resident the same story: this city has given up. Vision 2040 flips that narrative — with a citywide network of signature fountains, activated vacant lots, gateway monuments, a street tree canopy, and a public mural program that signals pride and forward momentum before a single new building goes up. Beauty is not an amenity. It is economic development.

Signature Infrastructure

El Dorado Fountain Network — 8 Water Landmarks

Fountains do something no sign can do: they stop people. A well-placed fountain with evening lighting creates a gathering point, a photo moment, and an emotional anchor for a neighborhood. El Dorado's oil heritage, lake identity, and prairie character give every fountain a story to tell. This plan proposes eight distinct water features distributed across the city — from a signature downtown centerpiece to interactive splash pads and quiet neighborhood courtyard fountains.

⛲

Central Plaza Signature Fountain

The centerpiece of the downtown plaza redesign — a three-tier illuminated fountain with programmable LED color sequences, visible from Central Avenue in both directions. Designed as the definitive "El Dorado photo moment." Evening programming includes synchronized water and light shows timed to the summer concert series. The plaza surface wraps around it with bench seating, shade trees, and food truck hook-ups. When this fountain runs, downtown feels alive.

📍 Central Ave Plaza 💰 Est. $380K 📅 Phase 1
🛢️

Stapleton Oil Heritage Fountain

Located at the expanded Kansas Oil Museum campus on E. Central Ave., this fountain draws directly from the 1915 discovery story. The design features a stylized oil derrick structure with water cascading down the lattice, lit amber and gold at night — referencing the oil fire's glow that announced the Stapleton No. 1 strike. An interpretive ring around the fountain's edge embeds key dates and quotes from the discovery into the stone. A landmark in its own right before tourists even enter the museum.

📍 Kansas Oil Museum 💰 Est. $290K 📅 Phase 1–2
🌀

North Gateway Roundabout Fountain

A water feature embedded in the central island of the North Gateway District's signature entry roundabout at the intersection of US-77 and the new district connector road. Visible from the interstate sightline. Simple, bold, and branded — a single vertical jet with a stainless-steel prairie grass sculpture rising from its center. Drought-tolerant plantings surround it. This fountain tells every traveler pulling off I-35: something is happening here. Maintenance-minimal design: recirculating system with below-grade equipment vault.

📍 North Gateway District 💰 Est. $195K 📅 Phase 1
🌊

Lake Overlook Fountain & Reflecting Pool

At the new West Marina plaza, a shallow 40-foot reflecting pool flanked by native prairie plantings frames a panoramic sightline to the open water of El Dorado Lake. A low-profile bubbling jet at the center keeps the water in motion and prevents stagnation. At sunset, the reflective surface mirrors the sky and the lake beyond — an effortless photo backdrop. Functions as a quiet counterpoint to the activity of the marina; the place you walk to after dinner at the lakeside restaurant. Accessible pathways and bench seating on all four sides.

📍 West Marina Plaza 💰 Est. $230K 📅 Phase 2
🏞️

Walnut River Cascade Feature

Where the proposed Walnut River Trail enters downtown near the new pedestrian bridge, a naturalistic stone cascade feature channels a controlled flow of water over locally quarried limestone into a small wading pool below. The sound of running water marks the transition from trail to city — an acoustic cue that rewards walkers and cyclists arriving from the greenway. Native riparian plantings (sedge, switchgrass, wild bergamot) frame the feature on three sides. Low-energy pump; designed to integrate with the river's natural character rather than compete with it. A meditation bench overlooks the cascade from the trail's final bend.

📍 Walnut River Trail Entry 💰 Est. $165K 📅 Phase 2
💦

Interactive Splash Pad — Centennial Park

A ground-level interactive splash fountain in Centennial Park's family zone — 30 water jets embedded flush in a gently sloped concrete pad with drainage built-in. Jets activate by motion sensor or push-button console; no standing water. Safe for all ages from toddlers through middle school. Open Memorial Day through Labor Day. Operating cost: water ($3,200/season) + pump maintenance ($1,800/season). The highest-use per-dollar amenity in parks research: a splash pad in a park of this size generates 300–600 user-visits on a typical summer Saturday. Zero lifeguard requirement. A city that has a splash pad keeps young families from driving to Wichita on summer afternoons.

📍 Centennial Park 💰 Est. $115K 📅 Phase 1
🎖️

Veterans Memorial Fountain

A formal reflecting pool and wall fountain at the El Dorado Veterans Memorial, replacing the existing static installation with an active water feature that brings sound and movement to the site. The granite memorial wall remains central; the fountain is positioned perpendicular to it so both elements share sightlines. In-ground lighting illuminates the water from below at dusk. American flags on the approach walk frame the arrival sequence. An annual Memorial Day lighting ceremony becomes the city's most dignified civic event of the year.

📍 Veterans Memorial 💰 Est. $210K 📅 Phase 1–2
🏘️

Neighborhood Pocket Fountains — 5 Locations

Five small courtyard-scale fountains sited in neighborhood parks, residential pocket plazas, and commercial node green spaces distributed across the city's residential quadrants. Each is a self-contained solar-assisted recirculating wall or pedestal fountain — modest in scale, high in presence. Locations prioritized in the North Gateway residential neighborhood, the West Gateway workforce housing district, the downtown residential corridor, the Lakeside Village community green, and the Senior Campus plaza. Identical maintenance specs so Public Works can service all five with a single parts inventory. Budget: $38,000 per installation.

📍 5 Neighborhood Locations 💰 Est. $190K total 📅 Phases 1–3
⛲
El Dorado Fountain Network — Total Investment: ~$1.78M across all phases

Eight water features. Eight gathering points. Eight photo moments shared on social media by visitors who become ambassadors. At $1.78M total, the fountain network costs less than a single mile of road reconstruction — and it works every single day, telling anyone who sees it that El Dorado is a city that invests in beauty.

Filling the Gaps

Vacant Lot Activation — Turn Eyesores Into Assets

El Dorado — like most small Kansas cities — has a scattering of vacant lots, abandoned foundations, and underused parcels woven through its neighborhoods and downtown. Each one is a gap in the urban fabric: a missed tax dollar, a drag on adjacent property values, and a visual signal of stagnation. This program converts them systematically — not all at once, but relentlessly, one lot at a time — into green space, productive land, and community assets.

🏛️ City Program

Strategic Land Bank

The city acquires vacant and tax-delinquent lots at low cost through the county treasurer's tax sale process, holds them in a city land bank, and releases them to qualified buyers — developers, nonprofits, neighbors — with deed restrictions ensuring productive use. The land bank gives the city first-mover advantage on infill sites before speculators snap them up and let them sit. Initial capitalization: $500K for a revolving acquisition fund. Target: 20 lots acquired in Year 1.

🌻 Community Program

Pocket Park Kit Program

For every vacant lot in a residential neighborhood that the city cannot immediately develop, install a standardized Pocket Park Kit: native wildflower seeding, a simple bench, a trash can, and a QR-coded heritage or nature sign. Cost per lot: $8,000–$15,000. Installation takes one weekend. A vacant lot that looks like a pocket park is no longer a vacant lot — it's a neighborhood amenity. Program targets 10 lots in Year 1, growing to 25 by Year 3.

🥕 Food Program

Community Garden Plots

Convert 6–8 vacant residential lots into managed community garden programs: 20–30 individual raised-bed plots per site, rented to residents at $35/season with water access, tool storage, and compost stations provided. Butler CC's horticulture and environmental science programs co-manage soil testing and garden planning, giving students a live outdoor laboratory. Waiting lists at comparable programs in Kansas run 40–80 people — demand is proven. A community garden on a vacant lot generates 12× more social visits than a manicured lawn on the same footprint.

🪑 Streetscape Program

Parklets — Parking to Plaza

Convert 8–12 underused parallel parking spaces on Central Avenue and adjacent commercial streets into raised parklet platforms: outdoor café seating, planter boxes, bike racks, and shade structures that extend the sidewalk into the street. Cost per parklet: $12,000–$22,000. Applied in clusters of two to three spaces, they transform dead blocks into active pedestrian zones. Businesses adjacent to parklets report a 15–25% increase in sidewalk foot traffic. Installed seasonally April–October; stored in the public works yard in winter.

🎨 Arts Program

Temporary Lot Market Plazas

Designate 3–4 larger vacant lots in the downtown core as licensed Temporary Market Plazas — graded, graveled, and fenced spaces available for weekend farmers markets, food truck rallies, pop-up retail, seasonal pumpkin and Christmas tree sales, and outdoor movie screenings. Infrastructure cost: $25,000–$45,000 per lot (grading + gravel + basic electrical + fence). The lots pay their own carrying costs through vendor licensing fees ($50–$150/event) while awaiting permanent development. They also prove the foot traffic potential to skeptical developers.

🌳 Neighborhood Program

Lot Adoption Initiative

Residents, businesses, and churches within 150 feet of a city-owned vacant lot can apply to adopt it: mowing, basic landscaping, and upkeep in exchange for zero-cost use as a side yard, garden, or passive green space — with a recorded city agreement giving the city the right to reclaim it for development. The adopted lot is maintained by someone who cares about it, and the neighbor gets effectively expanded outdoor space. No cost to the city. Self-sustaining once launched. Comparable programs in Topeka and Lawrence have activated 200+ lots.

First Impressions

Gateway Monuments — El Dorado's Front Doors

El Dorado is entered from four directions, and none of them announce arrival. A branded gateway monument at each primary entry point — I-35 Exit 71, US-54 westbound, US-77 northbound, and the downtown historic core — gives the city a signature identity visible from the road. Each monument tells the same story: you are somewhere. Cost per monument: $80,000–$150,000 in stone, steel, and landscaping. ROI: every visitor who photographs it becomes a social media post.

🛣️

I-35 / Exit 71 Monument

Positioned at the US-77 off-ramp from I-35 northbound — the highest-volume entry point. Backlit stone monument with the city name and "Est. 1857" and prairie grass plantings. Visible at highway speed. The first impression for every through traveler who knows nothing about El Dorado.

🌅

US-54 West Entry

The Wichita commuter gateway. A welcoming monument flanked by native flowering meadow plantings (blanketflower, coneflower, blue wild indigo) that bloom May–September. The meadow is a low-maintenance, high-visual-impact installation that makes the US-54 approach beautiful at every season. LED path lighting along the entry corridor after dark.

🛢️

US-77 North Entry

The road from Towanda and northern Butler County. An oil-heritage themed monument — a stylized derrick silhouette in weathering steel (Cor-Ten), naturally aging to a warm rust color that echoes the Flint Hills landscape. Requires zero painting or maintenance for 50+ years. Text: "El Dorado, Kansas — Oil Capital of the Great Plains."

🏛️

Historic Downtown Arch

A decorative arch spanning Central Avenue at the intersection marking the formal entry to the historic downtown district — anchoring the pedestrian experience and framing the streetscape view. Wrought iron and stone construction echoing the oil-boom era architecture of the avenue. LED uplighting at night. The functional equivalent of a downtown welcome sign that actually belongs on a historic commercial street.

Street-Level Beauty

Streetscaping, Lighting & Tree Canopy

Downtown Streetscape Package

Central Avenue from Main Street to the Oil Museum block deserves a complete streetscape investment: decorative lamp posts with hanging flower baskets (spring–fall), stamped and colored crosswalks at every intersection, street-level planters with seasonal plantings maintained by the Main Street program, and consistent bench and trash can hardware in a single branded design language. Cost for six city blocks: $850,000–$1.1M over Phase 1–2.

  • Decorative cast-iron lamp posts (replaces wooden poles) — heritage oil-era styling
  • Hanging flower baskets on every lamp post, May–October (600 baskets citywide by Phase 2)
  • Stamped concrete crosswalks at all Central Ave intersections
  • Consistent bench, bike rack, and waste receptacle standard (navy + gold powder coat)
  • Wayfinding kiosk system: 12 branded signs directing visitors to lake, museum, parks, dining
  • Sidewalk repair program: eliminate all cracked, uneven, or missing sidewalk sections within the Main Street district
  • Underground utility relocation: bury overhead lines on the 4 most prominent Central Ave blocks
  • Green infrastructure integration: bioswale planters at 6 downtown corner bump-outs capture stormwater runoff and replace standard concrete curbs with native-planted drainage channels — qualifying for EPA's "Greening America's Communities" free design assistance and reducing stormwater fees on adjacent properties
  • Permeable pavers in pedestrian plazas and crosswalk aprons: reduces stormwater runoff volume by 40–60% vs. standard concrete and qualifies for FEMA BRIC pre-disaster mitigation credits
  • Rain garden pilot at Central Plaza: a 600 sq ft depressed planting bed at the proposed central plaza captures 8,000+ gallons per storm event from the surrounding 1/4-acre hardscape — serving as a public demonstration site for the residential rain garden rebate program

Tree Canopy Expansion

El Dorado's residential streets and downtown core are underforested relative to comparable Kansas cities. A 100-trees-per-year program, maintained for 10 years, doubles the city's canopy cover — improving air quality, reducing summer heat, and increasing adjacent property values by 3–7% on tree-lined blocks (a measurable outcome documented in municipal tree research). Species priority: bur oak, chinkapin oak, lacebark elm, and Osage orange — all native or adapted Kansas species requiring no irrigation after establishment.

  • 100 street trees planted annually — starting Year 1 of Phase 1
  • Priority planting in North Gateway District (new development) and downtown (heritage blocks)
  • Tree grate installations in downtown sidewalk zone (protection + aesthetics)
  • Neighborhood tree give-away program: 200 native trees/year to residential landowners
  • Butler CC horticulture students manage the nursery stock and provide planting crews (reduced cost)
  • Memorial tree program: dedicate specific trees to community members, funded through donations
  • 10-year target: 1,000 new trees in the ground — permanent legacy of shade, beauty, and environmental value
Comfort & Invitation

Benches, Seating & Street Furniture — An Invitation to Stay

A city without places to sit is a city that is telling people to keep moving. Benches, planter boxes, shade pergolas, and consistent street furniture are not decorative extras — they are the infrastructure of public life. Every bench installed in downtown El Dorado is an investment in the five-minute conversation that turns a visitor into a repeat customer, a newcomer into a neighbor, and a passing resident into someone who stays. The standard should be: no point along Central Avenue is more than 150 feet from a place to sit.

🪑 Downtown Program

Central Avenue Bench Standard — 40 Seats on 6 Blocks

Install 40 matching bench-and-planter units along Central Avenue from Main Street to the Oil Museum approach — one pair every 75 feet on the primary pedestrian side, with additional units at the corner bump-outs and plaza areas. Hardware standard: cast-iron ends with reclaimed wood slats, powder-coated in navy and gold. Each unit includes an integrated planter box with seasonal flowers maintained by the Main Street program. The consistent hardware language creates the visual identity that signals "curated downtown" rather than "ad hoc street furniture." Cost per unit (bench + planter): $2,200–$3,400. Total 40 units: $110K–$140K installed.

🌿 Scenery Program

Planter Box Network — Seasonal Color at Every Turn

Deploy 120 large-format (30"×60") planter boxes at every intersection, every building entry, and every transition point along the main commercial and civic corridors. Spring/summer mix: geraniums, petunias, calibrachoa, and ornamental sweet potato vine. Fall: ornamental kale, mums, and rudbeckia. Winter: evergreen boughs, dogwood stems, and seasonal lighting. Maintained by a contracted local nursery under a city landscaping services agreement, eliminating the volunteer dependency that causes other cities' planter programs to fail in Year 2. 120 boxes × $600/season maintained: $72K/year operating. Visual impact: incalculable. A city that blooms every spring tells everyone who sees it that someone is in charge.

🌳 Shade Program

Shade Pergolas at Key Gathering Points

Install five permanent shade pergola structures at the highest-traffic outdoor gathering points in the city: Central Plaza (downtown), North Park, Centennial Park main shelter approach, McDonald Stadium southeast corner (shade for tournament spectators between games), and the West Marina overlook. Pergola design: steel frame with powder coat, cedar cross-members, integrated LED string lighting. Each pergola accommodates 12–20 people under cover and creates a defined "room" in outdoor space — transforming an open plaza into a place. Cost per pergola: $28,000–$45,000. Five structures: $165K–$225K total. Eligible for AARP Community Challenge grants (rapid livability improvement grants of $2,000–$50,000) — apply annually.

🎨 Art Program

Sculptural Seating — Art You Can Sit On

Commission four custom sculptural benches from Kansas artists — each sited at a high-visibility park or plaza location and designed to function as both seating and public art. Themes tied to El Dorado's identity: an oil derrick cross-section bench at the Kansas Oil Museum entrance; a "Walnut River fish" bench at the downtown river trail entry; a prairie grass wave bench at the North Gateway park; and a "Golden City" abstract form bench at Central Plaza reflecting El Dorado's Spanish name. Artist commissions: $8,000–$18,000 each. Functional art at this price point generates more social media engagement per dollar than any other public investment. These four pieces become the city's visual identity on Instagram before a single marketing budget is spent.

🌺 Scenery Program

Native Wildflower Meadow Corridors

Convert 14 acres of city-maintained mown turf along road rights-of-way, park borders, and utility easements into managed native wildflower meadows. Species mix: blanketflower (Gaillardia), purple coneflower, black-eyed Susan, wild bergamot, Kansas gayfeather, compass plant, and little bluestem grass. Meadows are mown once per year in late winter — reducing mowing cost by $380–$620 per acre per year versus maintained turf (total savings: $5,300–$8,700/year ongoing after Year 2 establishment). The meadows provide: monarch butterfly habitat (Waystation designation), native bee forage corridors, visual seasonal interest from April through November, and a visible symbol of El Dorado's prairie identity. Priority locations: US-54 west entry corridor, North Gateway park borders, Walnut River greenway edges, and the US-77 highway median.

💧 Scenery Program

Pocket Water Gardens — 6 Neighborhood Installations

Install six small-scale water garden features (each 6–12 ft diameter) at neighborhood park centers — naturalistic pond-style installations with water iris, pickerelweed, lotus, and water lilies in a recirculating gravel-filtration system. Each installation requires no chemicals and no heating — native cold-hardy plants, self-maintaining ecology, sealed liner. Cost per installation: $7,000–$14,000 installed. These are not formal fountains — they are naturalistic features that feel found rather than built. Placed at North Park, Harvey Park, South Park, Centennial Park south border, the North Gateway pocket park, and the Senior Campus courtyard. They provide the sound and movement of water in quiet neighborhood settings without the maintenance overhead of mechanical fountain systems. USDA Urban Agriculture and LWCF Challenge Cost-Share programs fund small-scale water feature and habitat installations.

Public Canvas

Mural & Public Art Program

El Dorado's blank building walls, grain elevator facades, utility boxes, and underpasses are untapped canvases. A commissioned mural program — led by the El Dorado Arts Council, funded jointly by the Main Street BID, business sponsors, and NEA grants — transforms dead surfaces into destinations. Research across dozens of mural cities shows that a large-scale mural on a blank downtown wall generates 500–2,000 incremental visits per year from people specifically seeking it out. Instagram does the rest.

🛢️ Oil Boom Heritage Mural

A large-scale (40×80 ft) mural depicting the 1915 Stapleton No. 1 discovery scene — derricks, workers, the moment of the strike — on the east-facing wall of the Kansas Oil Museum. Commissioned from a Kansas muralist. Target for the Mural Arts Festival anchor. A piece of genuine civic art that doubles as heritage tourism infrastructure.

🌾 Prairie & Lake Identity Mural

A panoramic prairie-to-water mural on the downtown parking garage's street-facing wall — native grasses, Flint Hills horizon, El Dorado Lake in the distance, and a great blue heron in the foreground. Visible from Central Avenue. Establishes the city's natural identity for every visitor who passes it. Funded through KCAIC's public art grant (up to $25K) and business sponsorship matching.

🎓 Butler CC Campus Gateway Mural

A mural commissioned for the main campus entry wall — celebrating the college's 1927 founding, its 100-year community impact, and the faces of El Dorado's working workforce. Co-designed by Butler CC art students as a capstone project. Text: "El Dorado Built This." A point of campus pride visible from Haverhill Road.

🏘️ Neighborhood Identity Murals (×4)

Four neighborhood-specific murals — one per residential quadrant — painted on prominent block walls and identified through a resident nomination process. Each depicts the specific character of that neighborhood. The nomination process itself builds community engagement and belonging. Budget: $12,000–$18,000 per mural including artist fee, materials, and prep.

🎨 Utility Box Art Program

Commission local and Butler CC student artists to paint every traffic signal control box and utility transformer box in the downtown district as mini-canvases. 30–40 boxes citywide. Cost: $400–$800 per box (student artist stipend). The effect is cumulative: a downtown with 35 painted utility boxes feels like a place where creativity is welcome. Kansas City, Wichita, and Lawrence all have active programs — El Dorado can launch one for under $25,000.

🎪 Annual Mural Arts Festival

A three-day summer festival anchored by a live mural painting competition in the downtown plaza — 8 artists, 8 walls, 72 hours, livestreamed. Vendors, food trucks, and live music surround the active painting sites. The finished murals become permanent. The festival brings 3,000–5,000 visitors the weekend it runs and generates ongoing social media coverage of the finished works for years after. Operating budget: $85,000/yr; target revenue through vendor fees and sponsorships: $55,000.

Mobility & Connectivity

Transportation Improvements

A city that is easy to get to — and easy to move around — attracts residents, workers, and investment. El Dorado has strong highway bones. The gaps are in first/last mile, Wichita connectivity, and sustainable mobility options.

🛣️

US-54 / US-77 Interchange Upgrade

The intersection of US-54 and US-77 is El Dorado's economic front door and handles significant freight traffic from the south industrial corridor. The current design creates dangerous conflict points and congestion during industrial shift changes. Upgrade to a modern diverging-diamond interchange with dedicated freight turning lanes, coordinated signal timing, and pedestrian refuge islands.

  • Diverging-diamond interchange design
  • Dedicated freight-priority turning lanes
  • Coordinated adaptive signal timing
  • Est. $14M — KDOT partnership (80% state)
  • Landscaped median and gateway welcome signage at city entry
  • Dedicated truck inspection pull-out for commercial vehicles
  • Emergency crossover and median break for incident management
  • Real-time traffic monitoring cameras integrated with KDOT network
  • Pedestrian refuge islands at all crosswalk approaches
  • LED roadway lighting on all interchange ramps
  • Wayfinding signs directing travelers to El Dorado Lake, downtown, and hospital
🚌

Wichita Express Bus Connection

Launch a weekday commuter bus route: El Dorado → Augusta → Wichita east side → downtown Wichita. Three morning/three evening runs. A Park-and-Ride facility at the US-54/77 junction with 200 covered spaces anchors the route. Price it at $5 each way / $80 monthly pass. Makes El Dorado viable for Wichita workers who can't afford metro rents — and positions it as a bedroom community alternative to Derby and Andover.

  • 200-space Park-and-Ride at US-54/US-77
  • 6 daily runs (3 AM inbound / 3 PM outbound)
  • $5 one-way; $80/month unlimited pass
  • Est. $3M capital + $600K/yr operating
  • Wi-Fi on all buses; USB charging at every seat
  • ADA-accessible low-floor vehicles — 100% accessible fleet
  • Real-time arrival tracking via Google Maps and Transit App integration
  • Employer-sponsored bulk pass programs: refinery, aerospace campus, hospital
  • Guaranteed connection times matched to Wichita Metro Area Transit network
  • TDM (Transportation Demand Management) program for major employers
  • Express premium tier: reserved seating, guaranteed departure
  • Annual ridership report to city commission and CMAQ federal funder
🚴

Connected Bike Network

20 miles of connected bicycle infrastructure — protected lanes on Central Avenue and along US-77 frontage roads, plus shared-use paths in residential areas. Connects downtown to El Dorado Lake, Butler CC, the sports complex, and all major residential zones. Includes 10 bike-share stations (30 e-bikes) and secure covered parking at the transit hub, library, and recreation center.

  • 8 miles protected bike lanes (downtown/arterial)
  • 12 miles shared-use path (residential/trail)
  • 10 bike-share stations with 30 e-bikes
  • Est. $4.5M — CMAQ federal grant eligible
  • Secured indoor bike parking at transit hub, library, and downtown parking
  • Bike repair stations with pumps every 1.5 miles on primary routes
  • County connector route linking El Dorado to Augusta bike trail
  • Designated safe bike-to-school corridors for all USD 490 campuses
  • Bicycle boulevard on low-volume residential street parallel to Central Ave
  • Wayfinding signs with distance and QR-linked trail maps at every intersection
  • Annual bike count and mode-share tracking for federal grant reporting
  • Flint Hills Gravel Route designation for touring cyclists (50+ miles)
⚡

EV Charging Network

Install 8 DC Fast Chargers and 40 Level-2 stations at strategic locations — downtown plaza, El Dorado State Park entrance, hospital campus, transit hub, industrial parks, north marina, and the data center site. Apply for NEVI Formula Program funding (Kansas received $39.5M over 5 years). Position El Dorado as the charge stop on the Wichita–Emporia corridor, capturing I-35/US-54 pass-through traffic.

  • 8 DC Fast Chargers (150kW each)
  • 40 Level-2 stations (citywide)
  • Open ChargePoint network (public access)
  • Est. $1.2M — 80% NEVI-funded
  • Workplace charging grant program: 50% city match for employers adding Level-2 stations
  • Hotel and marina destination charging at boutique hotel and lake amenity hub
  • School bus electrification pilot: 3 USD 490 buses converted in Phase 2
  • City fleet electrification: 10 EVs in city vehicle pool by 2028
  • EV-ready building code requirement for all new commercial construction
  • ChargePoint roaming integration for cross-Kansas EV network access
  • Real-time charger availability displayed on city website and navigation apps
  • Overnight RV electric hookups at north marina and Oil Museum lot
🚶

Safe Pedestrian Infrastructure

Conduct a citywide sidewalk audit, repair or install 6 miles of missing sidewalk links in school zones and along major arterials. Install HAWK pedestrian signals at 8 high-risk crossings. Build a pedestrian bridge over the Walnut River connecting east-side neighborhoods to downtown — eliminating a significant barrier that forces pedestrians onto a 4-lane road crossing to reach Central Avenue.

  • 6 miles new/repaired sidewalk
  • 8 HAWK pedestrian signal installations
  • Walnut River pedestrian bridge
  • Est. $3.8M — Safe Streets for All grant
  • Vision Zero policy adoption: target zero pedestrian/cyclist fatalities by 2035
  • School safe routes program: designated walking corridors to all USD 490 campuses
  • 200 non-ADA compliant curb ramp replacements citywide
  • Crosswalk countdown timers at all signalized intersections
  • LED lighting upgrade on all designated pedestrian corridors
  • Pedestrian volume counts integrated with smart sensor network
  • Accessible surface improvements along Walnut River greenway
  • Speed table installations in school zones and near senior campus
🚂

Freight Rail Advantage — BNSF & Union Pacific

El Dorado sits on the BNSF main line carrying 46 trains per day — one of the highest-frequency freight corridors in Kansas. Union Pacific maintains an industrial lead line from Wichita. Both Class 1 railroads provide El Dorado manufacturers direct access to national rail networks without short-line surcharges. Savage Services operates a multi-commodity transload terminal on the BNSF line (built 2011 with HollyFrontier Refining), handling liquid bulk, dry bulk, hazmat, steel, lumber, and container unloading. Union Tank Car Company (UTLX) — the largest railcar lessor in North America — operates its 40-acre El Dorado repair facility directly adjacent to the BNSF line. This rail infrastructure is a tier-1 site selection asset for any manufacturer requiring freight rail access.

  • BNSF main line: 46 trains/day — direct Class 1 national freight access
  • Union Pacific industrial lead from Wichita — second Class 1 carrier
  • Savage Services transload terminal on BNSF: liquid bulk, dry bulk, hazmat, container
  • UTLX railcar repair facility: 40 acres adjacent to BNSF, ~70 employees
  • No short-line interchange costs — direct Class 1 pricing for El Dorado shippers
  • BNSF transload facility within 5 miles of all industrial park sites
  • Rail-served industrial pad development: pre-extend rail spur to shovel-ready sites
  • Tank car, flatcar, boxcar, pneumatic, hopper, and container service all available
  • Hazmat-rated transload capability supports chemical, agricultural, and fuel industries
  • Rail access combined with I-35 highway: multi-modal freight package competitive with metro sites
  • Market BNSF connection in all industrial recruitment materials and LOIS site listings
✈️

El Dorado Airport (EQA) Upgrades

El Dorado's general aviation airport is an underused asset. Wichita's Dwight D. Eisenhower National Airport is increasingly congested for business aviation. Extend EQA's runway to 5,500 ft to accommodate larger business jets, add instrument approach capability (ILS), construct 20 new T-hangar units (high demand from Wichita aviation businesses needing overflow), and install 24-hour self-serve fuel. Position EQA as Wichita's business aviation relief valve.

  • Runway extension to 5,500 ft
  • ILS instrument approach installation
  • 20-unit T-hangar development (private)
  • Est. $7M — FAA AIP (90% federal)
  • 24-hour self-serve avgas and Jet-A fuel with automated credit card dispensing
  • Pilot lounge renovation: weather station, flight planning workstation, crew car
  • Customs and Border Protection facility feasibility study for international charter
  • On-airport car rental arrangement with regional agency
  • Airport beacon upgrade and approach lighting modernization
  • UAS (drone) corridor designation and testing zone partnership with Butler CC
  • Charter service marketing to data center and aerospace company executives
  • Rebranding: "El Dorado Regional Airport (EQA)" with updated signage and online presence
  • Fuel farm expansion to support increased traffic from renovated hangars
🚂

Amtrak Thruway — Newton Station Connector

Newton, Kansas — 30 miles west of El Dorado — is a functioning Amtrak Southwest Chief stop on one of Amtrak's busiest long-distance corridors (Chicago–Los Angeles). An Amtrak Thruway motor coach connection, Newton → Augusta → El Dorado Lake → Downtown, would place El Dorado on the national rail network without requiring a new rail line. Two daily runs aligned with Southwest Chief arrivals and departures. A dedicated El Dorado stop at the Lake Visitor Center and Downtown plaza. If a future Heartland Flyer expansion or new Midwest rail corridor materializes, El Dorado's US-54 position makes it a strong standalone Amtrak station candidate — the city should advocate proactively and build the political case now.

  • Amtrak Thruway motor coach: Newton → Augusta → El Dorado
  • 2 daily runs aligned with Southwest Chief schedule
  • Stops: El Dorado Lake Visitor Center + Downtown Plaza
  • Est. $280K/yr operating (KDOT Thruway subsidy eligible)
  • KDOT feasibility study — Phase 1 priority action
  • Long-range: standalone El Dorado Amtrak station advocacy
  • Passenger shelter, seating, and real-time display board at downtown stop
  • Amtrak Thruway ticketing integration — bookable on amtrak.com
  • Luggage and bicycle accommodation on all buses
  • Package tour with Visit Wichita and Visit Kansas for long-distance rail visitors
  • Seasonal pricing during summer festival weekends and El Dorado Lake events
  • Annual ridership targets tied to KDOT subsidy renewal criteria
  • Free downtown destination map given to all arriving Thruway passengers
🌉

Bridge Inventory — Repair, Beautification & New Crossings

El Dorado's bridges are both functional infrastructure and potential landmarks. The Walnut River runs through the heart of the city, and several key crossings are aging or aesthetically unremarkable. Vision 2040 addresses bridges on three fronts: safety and structural repair, aesthetic enhancement of existing crossings, and strategic new crossings that unlock connectivity for pedestrians and cyclists.

  • Walnut River Pedestrian Bridge (Downtown): New 180-ft signature pedestrian and bicycle bridge connecting east-side neighborhoods to the downtown core — eliminating the current four-lane barrier that forces walkers onto US-54 overpasses. Steel arch design with local limestone accent work; LED pathway lighting; viewpoint overlook deck at the midpoint; native riparian planting on both approaches. Est. $1.6M — Safe Streets for All federal grant.
  • Walnut River Greenway Footbridge Network: Three additional pedestrian footbridges (each 60–90 ft) at trail crossings along the Walnut River Greenway corridor — enabling a continuous off-road trail loop accessible to cyclists, pedestrians, and mobility-assisted users without any at-grade road crossings. Simple low-profile steel deck bridges with wood handrails; designed for durability over aesthetics. Est. $280K each.
  • El Dorado Lake Scenic Causeway Overlook: The existing causeway road at El Dorado Lake is a high-traffic route used by campers, boaters, and day visitors but offers no organized viewpoint or pull-off. Add a designated scenic overlook platform (20×40 ft concrete and stone cantilevered deck) at the highest-profile lake-view point on the causeway — with informational panels, bench seating, and a photographic framing structure. This costs $75K–$100K and creates the single most photographed viewpoint in Butler County.
  • Central Avenue / Walnut River Bridge Beautification: The existing vehicle bridge on Central Avenue over the Walnut River is structurally sound but visually blank. Add: decorative cast-iron railing panels in oil-derrick motif; limestone-capped parapet walls; LED downlighting on both sides illuminating the river below; and "Welcome to El Dorado" metal lettering integrated into the railing at the east approach. A $120K beautification investment that transforms a daily commuter crossing into a downtown landmark.
  • US-77 / Walnut River Bridge Upgrade: Schedule structural assessment and targeted repair of the US-77 bridge over the Walnut River — a freight and commuter artery that serves the industrial corridor. Coordinate with KDOT on the repair timeline and apply for IIJA Bridge Formula Program funds (100% federal for eligible bridges).
  • North Gateway District Connector Bridge: As the North Gateway residential area builds out, a new vehicular bridge over the Walnut River's north branch drainage channel will be required to connect the district's internal road network to existing streets east and west. Designed for 30-year traffic loads; include 10-ft shared-use path on one side. Coordinate with subdivision developer contributions and city TIF financing. Est. $480K.
  • Railroad Underpass Improvements: The BNSF rail crossings at-grade and the Main Street underpass beneath the rail corridor are visually bleak transitions that greet visitors arriving from the east side. Pressure-wash and paint underpass walls, install LED lighting on the underpass ceiling, and commission a mural on the eastern retaining wall that serves as a "welcome to downtown" signal for eastbound traffic on Central Avenue.
🚌

Year-Round Commuter + Seasonal Lake Express

El Dorado's transit strategy serves two distinct markets that require two distinct branded services. El Dorado Commuter Express serves the 1,500+ residents who commute daily to Wichita — 365-day weekday service, 6 runs per day, monthly pass pricing that makes the math easy. El Dorado Lake Shuttle serves visitors heading to the lake from Wichita, the ICT airport, and regional towns — Memorial Day through Labor Day, bike-rack equipped, priced for day-trippers. Both anchor at the US-54/US-77 Park-and-Ride hub with 200 covered EV-ready parking spaces and real-time arrival displays.

  • Commuter Express: 365-day service · 6 weekday runs (3 AM / 3 PM) · $80/mo pass
  • Lake Shuttle: Memorial Day–Labor Day · 4 daily runs · $6 one-way · bike rack
  • ICT Airport Connector: Seasonal · 4 daily runs · $12 round-trip · Wichita Eisenhower
  • Corporate pass program for aerospace, data center, and refinery commuters
  • Google Maps + Transit App real-time integration
  • Est. $3M capital + $900K/yr operating (50% CMAQ-eligible)
  • Mobile app ticketing with digital monthly pass and family fare options
  • Onboard climate control, Wi-Fi, and USB charging standard on all vehicles
  • Luggage and recreational equipment storage for lake visitors
  • Pet-friendly policy on Lake Shuttle with designated seating
  • Senior and disabled discount pass: 50% off monthly rate
  • Annual service review and route adjustment based on ridership data
  • Social media and concierge marketing partnership with Wichita hotels
  • Student pass: Butler CC students ride free with valid ID
Traffic Flow & Economic Corridors

Corridor Vitalization — Six Key Arteries

A city's corridors are its economic bloodstream. Cars, trucks, cyclists, and pedestrians flow through them constantly — and their visual quality, safety, and connectivity determine whether visitors stop and spend money, or drive through without a second glance. El Dorado has six primary corridors, each with distinct character and distinct potential. This section plans each as a complete streetscape experience — not just pavement, but trees, lighting, signage, landscaping, art, and activity that signal investment and invite engagement.

🏛️

Central Avenue Corridor — Downtown Heart

Central Avenue from Main Street to E. Central (Oil Museum block) is El Dorado's primary commercial and civic spine. The corridor carries a mix of daily commuter traffic, visitor access to downtown, and pedestrian activity that is currently poorly supported. Vision 2040 treats Central Avenue as a complete street: decorative lamp posts with hanging flower baskets, stamped crosswalks, bump-out parklets, canopy street trees in grates, and a redesigned Central Plaza fountain anchor. Signal timing optimization reduces stop-and-go congestion for through traffic while pedestrian phases are extended. A dedicated left-turn storage lane at the US-77/Central Ave intersection eliminates the primary source of peak-hour delay. Underground utility burial on the four most prominent blocks removes the visual clutter of overhead wires. Target: Central Avenue becomes a street that residents use voluntarily — not just to move through, but to be on.

$1.1M streetscape · Signal optimization $180K · Underground utilities $650K (Phase 2) · Maintained by Main Street BID
🛣️

US-77 North Corridor — Gateway & Industrial Access

US-77 from I-35 Exit 71 south to the Central Avenue intersection is El Dorado's highest-visibility entry corridor — the route every visitor from the interstate takes. It currently presents as a generic highway approach with minimal landscaping, aging signage, and no sense of arrival. Vision 2040 interventions: landscape median plantings on the US-77 median (native grasses and flowering perennials maintained by a TxDOT-style adopt-a-highway program); a signature gateway monument at Exit 71; wayfinding signage at every key turn directing visitors to lake, downtown, museum; and coordinated LED roadway lighting at the three major intersections between I-35 and downtown. The corridor also serves the North Gateway District's primary access road — it must function as both an interstate gateway and a neighborhood street simultaneously, which requires careful design of lane widths, crosswalks, and speed management north of the railroad crossing.

$385K landscaping + signage · $310K lighting upgrades · Coordination with KDOT US-77 maintenance agreement
🏭

Haverhill Road / North Industrial Corridor

Haverhill Road is the primary access route to the mega-site, the North Gateway industrial zone, and the AMAZONE manufacturing facility. AMAZONE (1220 N Haverhill Rd) is already generating daily freight and employee traffic — and the road they use reflects on El Dorado's industrial brand in the eyes of every supplier, visitor, and potential tenant who drives it. Investments: pavement overlay and intersection repair on the section from Central Ave to the mega-site boundary; industrial wayfinding signs directing freight to the transload terminal and mega-site entry; heavy-load capacity shoulder improvements supporting OOD (overdimensional) transport for large manufacturing deliveries; perimeter landscaping of the industrial park boundary visible from the road; and consistent LED area lighting at all major intersections. The corridor becomes El Dorado's "first impression" for every site selector and industry visitor — it must communicate competence and investment.

$520K road overlay + shoulders · $140K wayfinding + lighting · Coordinated with El Dorado Inc. site marketing materials
🏘️

Chase Street / East Side Residential Corridor

Chase Street and the parallel east-side grid serve El Dorado's established residential neighborhoods — the legacy housing stock east of the Walnut River that the plan targets for rehabilitation and investment through the Legacy Neighborhood Revitalization program. The east-side corridor has aging sidewalks, intermittent street trees, and minimal lighting on residential blocks. Vision 2040 upgrades: complete the sidewalk network on all residential blocks within the school safe routes program footprint (connecting El Dorado Middle School and El Dorado High School to surrounding neighborhoods); install pedestrian-scale LED lighting on the corridor's major cross-streets; plant 200+ street trees over five years along Chase, Vine, and parallel residential streets — creating the visible canopy that signals neighborhood investment to potential homebuyers; and install 30 "neighborhood art" bench-and-planter installations at key block midpoints through a partnership with Butler CC art students. The east side is El Dorado's most overlooked asset. With tree canopy and walkable streets, it becomes competitive with new suburban development at a fraction of the infrastructure cost.

$380K sidewalk completion · $120K LED lighting · $290K street trees (5-yr program) · $65K bench/planter installations
🌊

Walnut River Greenway Corridor — Nature Trail Spine

The Walnut River is El Dorado's greatest underutilized public asset after El Dorado Lake itself. The river runs through the heart of the city — through established neighborhoods, past downtown, and north toward the lake — but currently functions primarily as a flood-control channel rather than a community amenity. Vision 2040 transforms the river corridor into a continuous 6-mile linear park and trail: paved multi-use path on the east bank from the downtown pedestrian bridge north to the Walnut River campground area near the lake; three new pedestrian footbridges enabling east-west trail crossings without using vehicle roads; native riparian plantings (cottonwood, bur oak, buttonbush, wild bergamot) on both banks replacing invasive species; interpretive signage at key natural features; three formal access points with parking, restrooms, and trail-head facilities; kayak and canoe launch ramps at two locations; and habitat restoration in the widest floodplain sections creating wildlife corridors visible from the trail. The Walnut River Corridor becomes El Dorado's equivalent of the Riverwalk — a destination in itself, not just a functional drainage ditch.

$4.2M corridor-wide (Phases 1–3) · LWCF grants + EPA Recreation Economy for Rural Communities TA · City Parks budget phased contribution
🌅

US-54 / US-400 West Corridor — Wichita Commuter Gateway

US-54 from the Butler County line west to the Central Avenue intersection is the primary route used by El Dorado's 1,500+ daily Wichita commuters and by visitors from ICT Airport. It is also the corridor with the highest potential to attract commercial development as El Dorado's population grows — the US-54 frontage zone already has the zoning, utilities, and traffic counts to support a regional commercial node. Vision 2040 interventions: a welcoming gateway monument at the county/city limits boundary; native wildflower and ornamental grass median plantings from the city limits to the first traffic signal (highly visible to commuters at 55 mph); coordinated signage directing travelers to the Park-and-Ride facility and commuter bus stop; improved deceleration lanes at the Central Avenue intersection; and a commercial node plan for the US-54 frontage zone designating parcels for highway commercial development (gas, food, lodging) that capture pass-through spending currently skipping El Dorado entirely. A 2,500-car-per-day average daily traffic count on US-54 represents $35–$55M in annual potential commercial capture if one full-service interchange service area is developed.

$175K landscaping + signage · $280K intersection improvements · Commercial node plan $45K (planning phase) · KDOT US-54 maintenance coordination
Police · Fire · Emergency Medical

Public Safety Expansion

Growth without safety infrastructure is unsustainable. El Dorado's current police and fire capacity was designed for a city of 12,000. As population expands toward 18,000 and development extends north and south, response times and coverage become a genuine public safety concern — and a development liability.

35Current sworn officers
1Fire stations (downtown)
8 minAverage response time

🚔 Police Department

El Dorado Police Department

Current: approximately 35 sworn officers, 1 main station downtown. The existing station serves the city well for the current footprint. As the North Gateway District, West Gateway residential, and data center campus develop, coverage gaps will emerge on the city's western and northern perimeters.

  • North Gateway substation (shared space with Fire Station 2)
  • 15 additional sworn officers by 2030 (phased hiring)
  • Citywide body camera program
  • New CAD (Computer-Aided Dispatch) system
  • K-9 unit expansion (1 additional handler)
  • School resource officer for El Dorado Middle School
  • Traffic enforcement expansion (US-54 corridor)
  • Community policing program in North Gateway
  • Butler CC Criminal Justice pipeline: 5 hires/year
  • Public-facing crime data dashboard: real-time incident transparency map
  • Mental health co-responder program: licensed clinician on select dispatched calls
  • De-escalation and crisis intervention training — all officers, required annually
  • Neighborhood watch expansion to North Gateway and Lakeside Village districts
  • Strategic traffic enforcement detail on US-54 and US-77 commercial corridors
  • Automated license plate reader network: 6 cameras at strategic city entry points
  • Records management system upgrade — real-time data sharing with Butler County
  • Youth Police Academy — 2-week summer program for USD 490 students
  • Succession planning: 5 internal promotion candidates formally identified annually
  • Community advisory board — quarterly public town halls with Police Chief
  • Social media monitoring for early identification of community safety concerns
  • Bike patrol unit for downtown and lake trail corridors
  • SRO (School Resource Officer) program expansion to El Dorado Middle School
Needs: Substation · 15 officers · CAD upgrade

🚒 Fire Department

El Dorado Fire Department

Current: Fire Station 1 downtown, mix of career and volunteer personnel. The existing station provides acceptable response times to the city core, but a second station is critical as development expands north (North Gateway District) and south (data center campus, south industrial park).

  • Fire Station 2 (North Gateway) — top priority
  • Reduces north response time from 12+ min to <5 min
  • New 100-ft aerial/platform ladder truck
  • 4 additional career firefighters (Station 2 crew)
  • HAZMAT capability upgrade (chemical/industrial response)
  • ISO rating improvement: Class 4 → Class 3
  • Improved ISO saves residential insurance premiums citywide
  • Mutual aid agreement expansion with Augusta, Andover, Benton
  • Butler CC Fire Science program pipeline: 3 hires/year
  • Technical rescue team — trench rescue, high-angle, and water rescue cross-training
  • Drone program for aerial incident assessment and active search operations
  • Wildland-urban interface training: Flint Hills prairie fire response protocols
  • Medical first responder (MFR) cross-training for all career firefighters
  • Pre-incident planning for critical facilities: refinery, data center, hospital
  • Community risk reduction: 500+ home fire safety visits annually by firefighters
  • Residential sprinkler retrofit incentive: $500 rebate for voluntary sprinkler installation
  • Community CPR and Stop the Bleed training: 1,000 residents certified per year
  • Apparatus replacement schedule: preventive replacement every 15 years
  • Annual community open house during National Fire Prevention Week
  • Knox Box program: emergency key access for all commercial and multifamily buildings
  • Gas detection and confined space rescue equipment upgrade
  • Training tower construction at Station 2 for live fire and rope training
Needs: Station 2 · Ladder truck · HAZMAT

🚑 Emergency Medical Services

Butler County EMS

EMS in Butler County is provided at the county level. As El Dorado grows and El Dorado Lake sees increased recreational use (water emergencies, trail accidents, event injuries), the city should advocate for dedicated EMS resources and invest in supportive infrastructure.

  • ALS (Advanced Life Support) unit dedicated to lake district
  • AED network expansion: 30 new public AEDs (parks, trails, lake)
  • Telemedicine dispatch for rural Butler County calls
  • Water rescue capability at El Dorado Lake
  • Event medical plan for amphitheater (5,000+ attendance events)
  • Butler CC EMT/Paramedic program: 10+ grads/year feeding EMS
  • Quick-response unit at Fire Station 2 (north district)
  • Drone-deployed AED trial program (Butler CC tech partnership)
  • Community paramedic program: home visits for high-frequency 911 callers to address root causes
  • Mobile integrated health (MIH): chronic disease management home visits for seniors
  • STEMI (heart attack) receiving center protocol formalized with SBA Hospital
  • Stroke response telemedicine consult initiated on every suspected CVA call
  • Mass casualty incident supply cache: equipment and triage supplies for 50+ patients
  • Pediatric-specific resuscitation equipment on all ALS units — infant through adolescent
  • Mental health crisis response team: trained co-responders for behavioral health calls
  • Annual EMS performance report: response time, patient outcomes, and call volume by type
  • EMS–SBA Hospital information system integration for seamless patient hand-off
  • Reserve corps program: 10 EMT volunteers trained, credentialed, and drill-ready
  • Mass gathering event medical plan for amphitheater events (5,000+ attendance)
  • Hazmat medical surveillance protocol for refinery and data center incidents
Good: County EMS structure

Fire Station 2 — North Gateway: The Non-Negotiable

Fire Station 2 is not a nice-to-have. It is the infrastructure prerequisite for North Gateway District development. Insurance underwriters assess residential construction risk based on ISO ratings and response times — a 12-minute response time from Station 1 to the north gateway will increase homeowner insurance premiums by 15–25%, reducing the financial viability of the $45M residential investment in that district. Station 2 costs approximately $4.5M (land + construction + equipment). It should be included in Phase 1 as an enabling investment, not deferred to Phase 3 after the problem becomes acute.

Fiscal Analysis

Tax Implications & Fiscal Impact

Every project in this plan has tax implications — both costs and revenues. Understanding the fiscal math is essential for responsible planning. The key finding: the long-term tax base growth substantially exceeds the public investment required to generate it.

Property Tax Impact

New development adds to the city's assessed valuation base without requiring a proportional increase in the mill levy — because many costs (infrastructure) are one-time, while the tax revenue is permanent.

  • 1,200+ new housing units (avg $120K market value): adds ~$36M assessed value
  • At ~35 mills: ~$1.26M in new annual property tax revenue
  • Commercial/industrial development (1M+ sq ft): adds ~$40M assessed value
  • Data center equipment: exempt under SB 98, but real property is taxable
  • 300-acre data center campus real property: ~$30M assessed, ~$1.05M/yr
  • Net Phase 4 property tax increase vs. 2024 baseline: estimated +$4.8M/yr
  • Butler County and USD 490 share in the increase via county-wide assessment
  • Downtown boutique hotel (35 rooms): ~$3.5M assessed, generating ~$122K/yr in new property tax
  • West Side Marina (city-owned real property): assessed ~$5M, generating ~$175K/yr
  • AgriTech Hub campus: ~$8M assessed commercial value upon full buildout
  • Agricultural land converted to residential: typically 3–5× increase in assessed value per acre
  • TIF districts capture property tax increment for 20 years, then release full base to taxing entities
  • Homestead exemptions for senior residents maintained unchanged throughout all plan phases
  • Industrial Revenue Bond (IRB) eligible projects: 10-year property tax abatement on qualifying manufacturing equipment
  • As assessed value base grows, the mill levy can be held flat or reduced while still generating more total annual revenue
  • 2023 mill levy: 161.683–165.583 per $1,000 assessed value; commercial assessed at 25% of appraised value
  • All machinery & equipment: exempt from Kansas personal property tax since 2006 — a permanent competitive advantage for any manufacturer or data center
  • No business license fees in El Dorado — zero annual registration costs for any operating business

Sales Tax Revenue Projections

Tourism development (lake, downtown, events) generates taxable retail and restaurant sales. New rooftops generate grocery, service, and daily retail sales. El Dorado's combined sales tax rate is approximately 8.5%.

  • 2024 baseline: est. $3.2M annual city sales tax
  • Lake tourism (200K visitors/yr @ $40 avg taxable spend): +$680K
  • Downtown retail/restaurant activation: +$420K
  • New residential rooftops (1,200 units): +$1.5M
  • Industrial payroll spending (1,300 jobs): +$1.2M
  • Projected 2040 annual city sales tax: ~$7.0M (+$3.8M vs. 2024)
  • Retailer recruitment: every new major retailer adds $200–500K
  • Marina fuel sales (~100,000 gallons/yr): ~$60K in taxable sales per year
  • Craft brewery/taproom: average $800K–$1.2M in annual taxable sales
  • Sporting goods store (lake/outdoor focus): $400–600K in annual taxable sales
  • Hotel transient guest tax (10%): 35-room boutique at 70% occupancy generates ~$90K/yr
  • Amphitheater concessions and ticketing (~20 events/yr): $180–320K in taxable sales annually
  • Golf club food & beverage revenue: ~$400K annual restaurant sales at new clubhouse
  • Each new restaurant generates average $40–65K in annual city sales tax
  • Car dealership (if recruited): $600K–$1.2M in annual sales tax to city and county
  • Annual Boat Show (3-day event): ~$80K in city sales tax from vendor and visitor sales

Tax Abatements & Incentives

Short-term cost for long-term gain. Tax abatements are investments — every major Kansas economic development success story (Textron Aviation, Spirit AeroSystems/Boeing, Cerner) involved substantial public incentives. The math works when the abatement period is finite and the jobs are permanent.

  • Data center equipment: $50M+ in sales tax exemptions (state law; no city cost)
  • Renewable energy: 10-year personal property abatement on new industrial equipment
  • Aerospace campus: 5-year 50% property tax abatement (Phase 2–3)
  • Historic rehab (downtown): 25% state income tax credit on qualified rehab costs
  • New Market Tax Credits: 39% credit on qualifying community investments
  • Payback analysis: every $1 in abatement generates est. $4.20 in tax revenue by year 15
  • All abatements are finite — full tax revenue resumes permanently after the abatement period expires
  • IRB (Industrial Revenue Bond) structure: city is conduit issuer only, no city debt obligation created
  • STAR Bond districts: sales tax diverted to bond repayment during bond term, then fully released to city
  • Opportunity Zone investors receive federal capital gains deferral — zero direct cost to city or county
  • Downtown TIF funds facade grants and vacancy activation — these are not property tax exemptions
  • Kansas Dept. of Commerce approval required for economic development abatements exceeding $250K/yr
  • Annual abatement impact report to city commission: jobs created, wages paid, and compliance verified
  • Clawback provisions in all abatement agreements: early exit triggers proportional repayment

TIF Districts & Special Financing

Tax Increment Financing (TIF) allows the city to capture the property tax increment above a base assessment and redirect it to pay for public infrastructure in the district — without raising the mill levy or spending general fund dollars.

  • Downtown TIF District: 20-year, captures $2M+ in increment over life
  • North Gateway TIF: 20-year, funds road/utility/park infrastructure
  • South Industrial TIF: 15-year, funds pad-ready site preparation
  • STAR Bond eligibility: lake/downtown tourism anchors (Oil Museum, amphitheater)
  • Special Benefit District: streetscape and lighting on Central Avenue
  • Revenue bonds: marina, utilities (repaid from facility revenue)
  • TIF can fund public parking structures, avoiding the need for a GO bond and voter approval
  • Maximum TIF bond term under Kansas law: 20 years from district creation date
  • Annual TIF increment reporting to Kansas Department of Commerce is required by law
  • USD 490 and Butler County must be notified of TIF creation and may negotiate participation rates
  • Multiple TIF districts can operate simultaneously with separate increment accounts
  • TIF does not raise anyone's existing taxes — it redirects new tax growth from new development only
  • STAR Bond threshold: minimum $50M total project investment required for Kansas approval
  • Post-TIF expiration, all incremental assessed value flows permanently to city, county, and school district
  • TIF district performance review at years 5, 10, and 15 to adjust bond issuance as needed
  • Hotel/Motel transient guest tax (new revenue): 10% on overnight stays, dedicated to tourism fund

Kansas-Specific Programs That Help

El Dorado is not starting from scratch — Kansas has funding and incentive programs specifically designed for cities of its size and profile. Using all available tools is not optional; it's essential to making the fiscal math work.

  • Federal Opportunity Zone — downtown census tract; investor capital gains deferral for 5–10+ year commitments (Butler County does not qualify for the Kansas state ROZ program)
  • STAR Bonds — divert sales tax from tourism anchors to repay capital costs
  • Kansas Angel Investment Tax Credit — supports startup ecosystem
  • Kansas Industrial Training (KIT) — employer-paid workforce training
  • Kansas Industrial Retraining (KIR) — retrain displaced workers
  • IMPACT program — funding mechanism for Butler CC employer training

School District (USD 490) Impact

Population growth adds students; the Kansas school finance formula provides state aid per pupil. Industrial and commercial growth adds to the county-wide assessed valuation used in school finance calculations.

  • +500 students by 2035 generates ~$4.2M in state per-pupil aid
  • Industrial assessed value growth: +$22M added to USD 490 base
  • New elementary school may be needed by Phase 3 (~$18M, GO bond)
  • Data center: taxable real property provides school mill levy revenue
  • Butler CC dual enrollment reduces per-student cost in upper grades
  • Net: strongly positive for school finance without requiring a mill levy increase

How It Gets Paid For

Funding Strategy

No single source funds a plan this ambitious. The strategy layers federal grants, state programs, private investment, and locally generated mechanisms so each project is viable independently — not contingent on the whole plan succeeding first.

🏆

State Funding Already Awarded to El Dorado

El Dorado has already demonstrated the state partnership capability this plan requires. These recent wins prove the playbook works:

$3,000,000
B.A.S.E. Grant — Kansas Dept. of Commerce
Building a Stronger Economy grant awarded for water infrastructure improvements, industrial site certification, and sanitary sewer system upgrades. Exactly the industrial-ready infrastructure needed to recruit Vision 2040's manufacturing and data center targets.
$50,000
ROOMS Program — Kansas Dept. of Commerce
Residential Opportunities on Main Street grant awarded to add 13 apartments to 124 Lofts — the historic Masonic Lodge building downtown — with a planned ground-floor restaurant or retail space. El Dorado's first confirmed upper-floor residential conversion on Central Avenue, announced February 2026.
Active
El Dorado Main Street Program
Façade Improvement Grant Program, zero-interest Investment Without Walls loans (max $20,000 per applicant), window and design assistance, and business recruitment services — all currently active.
Underway
Velocity Fiber Deployment (Butler Electric Cooperative)
Fiber-to-the-home rollout already begun on El Dorado's west and north sides — the exact corridors that serve the North Gateway District and West Gateway residential zones. No new procurement needed for Phase 1 connectivity.
Approved
Data Center Zoning Ordinance — June 2026
City Commission unanimously approved a new zoning ordinance establishing modern standards for data centers and large-scale battery storage. No specific project is under review — the ordinance means El Dorado is code-ready to respond to any hyperscale developer inquiry without delays. City Manager David Dillner and Mayor Bill Young committed to full transparency if a specific project enters discussion.
2026
2026–2030 Capital Improvement Plan
Public hearing held March 3, 2025. The 5-year CIP sets the city's infrastructure investment schedule. Vision 2040's infrastructure projects — Central Avenue streetscape, water treatment upgrades, Walnut River flood mitigation, athletic complex improvements — should be positioned within the CIP framework during annual updates.
Open 2025
Gold City Hemp — New Downtown Main Street Business
Hemp-derived CBD products combined with an art gallery — the type of experience-plus-retail concept that draws foot traffic and dwell time that traditional downtown retail alone cannot. Demonstrates Central Avenue's viability for experiential retail tenants.

Sources: Kansas Dept. of Commerce, City of El Dorado, KWCH, Butler County Times-Gazette, Velocity/Butler Electric Cooperative — programs active as of 2025–2026

📋 El Dorado's Current Incentive Stack

Industrial Revenue Bonds (IRBs) Up to 85% property tax abatement for 10 years + building materials and equipment exempt from sales tax
Economic Development Exemptions (EDX) Up to 85% property tax abatement for 10 years on qualifying commercial and industrial projects
Community Improvement Districts (CID) Up to 2% retail sales tax or special assessment for up to 22 years — usable for commercial, industrial, and mixed-use capital improvements
Investment Without Walls Loans $0–$20,000 zero-interest loans for building purchase, renovation, or business operations — available now through El Dorado Main Street
Sales Tax Capital Fund 1-cent sales tax generates ~$700K/yr to streets; revenue exceeding $2.35M threshold goes to approved capital projects — a proven, voter-approved mechanism
PEAK Program (State) Retain/refund 95% of payroll withholding tax for qualified new employees — one of Kansas's most powerful recruitment incentives for large employers

Federal Programs

The federal government has unprecedented infrastructure and economic development funding available. El Dorado should retain a dedicated grant writer or contract with a firm specializing in rural community development grants.

  • BEAD Program — $42.5B for broadband ($12–16M target)
  • RAISE Grants — transportation projects (interchange, bike)
  • EDA Public Works — industrial site preparation
  • NEVI Program — EV infrastructure (80% cost share)
  • EPA WIFIA — water infrastructure low-interest loans
  • FEMA BRIC — flood mitigation (75% cost share)
  • FAA AIP — airport improvements (90% federal)
  • USDA ReConnect — rural broadband
  • HUD CDBG — community development block grants
  • IRA Investment Tax Credits — solar/energy projects
  • Safe Streets for All — pedestrian infrastructure
  • CMAQ — bike network, transit (80% cost share)
  • DoE Loan Programs Office (LPO) — clean energy manufacturers, up to $5B per project
  • 45X Advanced Manufacturing Production Credit (IRA) — solar, wind, and battery manufacturers
  • 45Q Carbon Capture Tax Credit (IRA) — refinery partnership, up to $85/ton CO₂ captured
  • HRSA Rural Health Grant Program — SBA Hospital expansion and rural health clinic
  • Federal Historic Tax Credits (20%) — all qualifying downtown adaptive reuse projects
  • National Endowment for the Arts (NEA) — amphitheater, arts district, and public art program
  • USDA Business & Industry Loan Guarantee — rural business lending support
  • SBA 7(a) and 504 Loan Programs — downtown businesses and small manufacturers
  • BIL (Bipartisan Infrastructure Law) formula funds — distributed annually through KDOT
  • EB-5 Immigrant Investor Program — $800K–$1.05M per investor in targeted employment areas
  • DoE BRIC Grants — building resilience for the community microgrid and grid modernization
  • USDA REAP (Rural Energy for America Program) — 25% grant + 75% guaranteed loan for rural renewable energy projects; covers wind, solar, and energy efficiency upgrades for businesses
  • USDA Value-Added Producer Grants (VAPG) — planning and working capital grants ($75K–$250K) for agricultural producers adding value through processing, marketing, or distribution; directly funds the food manufacturing hub and AgriTech startup
  • EPA Brownfields Multipurpose Grant — up to $800K for assessment and cleanup of contaminated former industrial sites; essential for south industrial corridor redevelopment and any legacy oil-field site conversion
  • HUD Section 108 Loan Guarantee — borrow up to 5× CDBG entitlement for economic development, housing rehabilitation, and public facilities; currently the largest single federal loan guarantee available to small cities
  • DOT BUILD Grant (Better Utilizing Investments to Leverage Development) — $5M–$25M for multimodal transportation infrastructure; covers US-54/US-77 interchange upgrade, Central Avenue reconstruction, and freight connector roads
  • HHS CCBHC Expansion Grant — Certified Community Behavioral Health Center planning and expansion grants ($1–2M) specifically for comprehensive community mental health and substance use services in underserved areas; directly funds behavioral health wing expansion
  • EDA Good Jobs Challenge — up to $25M for workforce training systems connecting education, employers, and workers in economic recovery regions; directly funds Butler CC expansion programs tied to specific employer commitments
  • DOE Industrial Demonstrations Program — up to $35M for decarbonization technology demonstrations in hard-to-abate industries; HF Sinclair refinery is a primary eligible applicant for carbon capture and hydrogen co-processing demonstrations
  • Treasury CDFI Fund — competitive grants ($5M–$25M) for establishing Community Development Financial Institutions providing capital in underserved markets; El Dorado could help establish a Butler County CDFI to fund small business loans, microenterprise, and homeownership in underserved areas
  • USDA Water and Waste Disposal Loan & Grant Program — combines grants (up to 75% for lowest-income rural areas) with long-term loans at 1.375–3.375% for water system construction and improvement; directly applicable to water treatment plant modernization and rural water line extensions
  • USDA Rural Business Development Grant (RBDG) — $10K–$500K for technical assistance, training, and economic development for rural small businesses; funds the business incubator, AgriTech hub feasibility work, and food manufacturing technical assistance
  • AmeriCorps VISTA — free-of-charge anti-poverty volunteer program placing dedicated full-time staff with local nonprofits; could provide grant writing, neighborhood organizing, and program coordination support at near-zero cost

State of Kansas Programs

Kansas has targeted funding tools for exactly the projects this plan proposes. Many are underutilized by communities that don't know they exist or don't have staff to pursue them.

  • KDOT Enhancement Grants — trails, bike lanes
  • Kansas Historic Preservation Tax Credits (25%)
  • Kansas Housing Resources Corp — workforce housing
  • KDWPT Concession Program — lake development
  • KDOC CDBG — small city economic development

  • Federal Opportunity Zone (downtown census tract) — capital gains incentive for qualifying investors
  • Kansas Angel Investment Tax Credit
  • KIT / KIR — workforce training cost share
  • IMPACT (via Butler CC) — employer training
  • SB 98 data center exemptions
  • Kansas Main Street Program — technical assistance for downtown facade improvements
  • Kansas Creative Arts Industries Commission (KCAIC) — arts district and amphitheater grants
  • Kansas Outdoor Recreation Economy (ORE) grants — trails, disc golf, and lake facilities
  • Kansas New Entrepreneurs Fund — financing for downtown incubator graduates
  • Kansas SBIR/STTR Bridge Fund — AgriTech Hub and cleantech startup support
  • Butler Community College Bond — North Gateway satellite campus financing
  • Kansas Water Authority (KWA) matching grants — water treatment and flood mitigation
  • KDOT T-WORKS Enhancement Program — Central Avenue streetscape and trail network
  • Kansas Enterprise Facilitation Program — rural small business coaching
  • Kansas Volunteer Firefighters Relief Association grants — fire equipment funding
  • KDOT aviation enhancement grants — EQA runway and hangar improvements
  • STAR Bond District — Sales Tax and Revenue Bonds; El Dorado Lake tourism anchor qualifies for a state-designated STAR Bond District in which 6.5% of sales tax increment generated within the district repays the bonds over 20 years; Kansas Speedway used this exact mechanism for $95M in development; requires Kansas Commerce designation and a tourism impact study proving regional visitor draw
  • Kansas Business Development Fund — low-interest loans (below prime rate) for qualifying small and mid-size businesses expanding or relocating in Kansas; administered by KDOC; complements SBA 7(a) for businesses that don't qualify for traditional bank financing
  • KDHE Brownfields State Response Program — state-level technical assistance and matching funds for contaminated site assessment; pairs with EPA Brownfields grants to provide complete site assessment and cleanup funding at near-zero local cost
  • Kansas Works Skills Development Program — employer-driven customized workforce training reimbursement; pays 50–75% of training costs for new or retained employees; pairs with Butler CC IMPACT program for maximum employer training subsidy
  • Kansas Department of Agriculture — Value-Added Agriculture grants and Specialty Crop Block Grants; funds feasibility studies and startup capital for food processing, craft production, and specialty agricultural product development; directly supports the food manufacturing and craft beverage district
  • Kansas VentureKAN — seed-stage technology venture fund with co-investment from Kansas Commerce; targets early-stage Kansas technology companies; relevant for AgriTech, cleantech, and health tech startups locating at the innovation campus
  • KDOT Transit Development Grant — capital grants for transit vehicle purchases and facility improvements; allows BCRTA to expand El Dorado service with minimal local match
  • Kansas Film Commission Incentives — 30% transferable tax credit on qualifying film production expenditures (proposed; vetoed 2024; re-introduction expected 2025–26); when enacted, positions El Dorado's downtown studio as a competitive Kansas production location
  • Kansas Outdoor Recreation Economy (ORE) Partnership Program — matching grants for trail development, recreation infrastructure, and outdoor tourism programming; directly supports 26-mile lake trail, disc golf, and paddler's trail network
  • USD 490 School Improvement Bond Capacity — El Dorado's school district can issue general obligation bonds approved by voters; a successful school bond reduces demand on city general revenue for education capital needs, freeing city resources for Vision 2040 investments; the district's declining enrollment may make bond approval more achievable if tied to specific building improvements
